<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Virtual reality</h1>

<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:HTC_Vive_(16).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3456" data-file-width="5184" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/HTC_Vive_%2816%29.jpg/220px-HTC_Vive_%2816%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/HTC_Vive_%2816%29.jpg/330px-HTC_Vive_%2816%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/HTC_Vive_%2816%29.jpg/440px-HTC_Vive_%2816%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:HTC_Vive_(16).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A person wearing a virtual reality headset called the "<a href="/wiki/HTC_Vive" title="HTC Vive">HTC Vive</a>". It was developed in co-production between <a href="/wiki/HTC" title="HTC">HTC</a> and <a href="/wiki/Valve_Corporation" title="Valve Corporation">Valve Corporation</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><b>Virtual reality</b> (<b>VR</b>) typically refers to <a href="/wiki/Computer" title="Computer">computer</a> technologies that use <a href="/wiki/Software" title="Software">software</a> to generate realistic images, sounds and other sensations that replicate a real environment (or create an imaginary setting), and simulate a user's physical presence in this environment, by enabling the user to interact with this space and any objects depicted therein using specialized <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flatscreen_TV" title="Flatscreen TV">display screens</a> or projectors and other devices. VR has been defined as "...a realistic and immersive simulation of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Three-dimensional" title="Three-dimensional">three-dimensional</a> environment, created using interactive software and <a href="/wiki/Computer_hardware" title="Computer hardware">hardware</a>, and experienced or controlled by movement of the body"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> or as an "immersive, interactive experience generated by a computer".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> A person using virtual reality equipment is typically able to "look around" the artificial world, move about in it and interact with features or items that are depicted on a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flatscreen_TV" title="Flatscreen TV">screen</a> or in goggles. Virtual realities artificially create sensory experiences, which can include sight, touch, hearing, and, less commonly, smell. Most 2016-era virtual realities are displayed either on a <a href="/wiki/Computer_monitor" title="Computer monitor">computer monitor</a>, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Projector_screen" title="Projector screen">projector screen</a>, or with a <a href="/wiki/Virtual_reality_headset" title="Virtual reality headset">virtual reality headset</a> (also called <a href="/wiki/Head-mounted_display" title="Head-mounted display">head-mounted display</a> or HMD). HMDs typically take the form of head-mounted goggles with a screen in front of the eyes. Some simulations include additional sensory information and provide sounds through speakers or headphones.</p>
<p>Some advanced <a href="/wiki/Haptic_technology" title="Haptic technology">haptic</a> systems in the 2010s now include tactile information, generally known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Force_feedback" title="Force feedback">force feedback</a> in medical, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Video_gaming" title="Video gaming">video gaming</a> and military training applications. Some VR systems used in video games can transmit vibrations and other sensations to the user via the <a href="/wiki/Game_controller" title="Game controller">game controller</a>. Virtual reality also refers to remote communication environments which provide a virtual presence of users with through <a href="/wiki/Telepresence" title="Telepresence">telepresence</a> and <a href="/wiki/Telexistence" title="Telexistence">telexistence</a> or the use of a <a href="/wiki/Virtual_artifact" title="Virtual artifact">virtual artifact</a> (VA), either through the use of standard input devices such as a keyboard and mouse, or through <a href="/wiki/Multimodal_interaction" title="Multimodal interaction">multimodal</a> devices such as a <a href="/wiki/Wired_glove" title="Wired glove">wired glove</a> or <a href="/wiki/Omnidirectional_treadmill" title="Omnidirectional treadmill">omnidirectional treadmills</a>. The immersive environment can be similar to the real world in order to create a <a href="/wiki/Lifelike_experience" title="Lifelike experience">lifelike experience</a>—for example, in simulations for pilot or combat training, which depict realistic images and sounds of the world, where the normal <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Laws_of_physics" title="Laws of physics">laws of physics</a> apply (e.g., in <a href="/wiki/Flight_simulator" title="Flight simulator">flight simulators</a>), or it can differ significantly from reality, such as in VR video games that take place in fantasy settings, where gamers can use fictional <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Magic" title="Magic">magic</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Telekinesis" title="Telekinesis">telekinesis</a> powers.</p>
<p></p>
