<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology_and_terminology">Etymology and terminology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Virtual_reality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology and terminology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:502px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Linux_kernel_and_gaming_input-output_latency.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1080" data-file-width="1920" height="281" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Linux_kernel_and_gaming_input-output_latency.svg/500px-Linux_kernel_and_gaming_input-output_latency.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Linux_kernel_and_gaming_input-output_latency.svg/750px-Linux_kernel_and_gaming_input-output_latency.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Linux_kernel_and_gaming_input-output_latency.svg/1000px-Linux_kernel_and_gaming_input-output_latency.svg.png 2x" width="500"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Linux_kernel_and_gaming_input-output_latency.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Paramount for the sensation of <a href="/wiki/Immersion_(virtual_reality)" title="Immersion (virtual reality)">immersion</a> into virtual reality are a high <a href="/wiki/Frame_rate" title="Frame rate">frame rate</a> (at least 95 fps), as well as a low <a href="/wiki/Latency_(engineering)" title="Latency (engineering)">latency</a>. Furthermore, a pixel persistence lower than 3 <a href="/wiki/Millisecond" title="Millisecond">ms</a> is required, because if not, users will feel sick when moving their head around.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1938, <a href="/wiki/Antonin_Artaud" title="Antonin Artaud">Antonin Artaud</a> described the illusory nature of characters and objects in the <a href="/wiki/Theatre" title="Theatre">theatre</a> as <a class="extiw" href="https://fr.wikipedia.org/wiki/R%C3%A9alit%C3%A9_virtuelle" title="fr:Réalité virtuelle">"la réalité virtuelle"</a> in a collection of essays, <i><a class="extiw" href="https://fr.wikipedia.org/wiki/Le_Th%C3%A9%C3%A2tre_et_son_double" title="fr:Le Théâtre et son double">Le Théâtre et son double</a></i>. The English translation of this book, published in 1958 as <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Theater_and_its_Double" title="The Theater and its Double">The Theater and its Double</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> is the earliest published use of the term "virtual reality". The term "<a href="/wiki/Artificial_reality" title="Artificial reality">artificial reality</a>", coined by <a href="/wiki/Myron_W._Krueger" title="Myron W. Krueger">Myron Krueger</a>, has been in use since the 1970s. The term "virtual reality" was used in <i>The Judas Mandala</i>, a 1982 science fiction novel by <a href="/wiki/Damien_Broderick" title="Damien Broderick">Damien Broderick</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Oxford_English_Dictionary" title="Oxford English Dictionary">Oxford English Dictionary</a> cites a 1987 article titled "<i>Virtual reality</i>",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> but the article is not about VR technology. "Virtual" has had the meaning "being something in essence or effect, though not actually or in fact" since the mid-1400s, "...probably via sense of "capable of producing a certain effect" (early 1400s)".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-etymonline.com_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-etymonline.com-5">[5]</a></sup> The term "virtual" was used in the "[c]omputer sense of "not physically existing but made to appear by software" since 1959.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-etymonline.com_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-etymonline.com-5">[5]</a></sup> The term "reality" has been used in English since the 1540s, to mean "quality of being real," from "French réalité and directly Medieval Latin realitatem (nominative realitas), from Late Latin realis".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>Virtual reality is also called "virtual realities", "immersive multimedia", "augmented reality" (or AR), "artificial reality" <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> or "computer-simulated reality". A dictionary definition for "<a href="/wiki/Cyberspace" title="Cyberspace">cyberspace</a>" states that this word is a synonym for "virtual reality".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Virtual_reality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Before_the_1950s">Before the 1950s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Virtual_reality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Before the 1950s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sensorama-morton-heilig-virtual-reality-headset.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="625" height="282" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Sensorama-morton-heilig-virtual-reality-headset.jpg/220px-Sensorama-morton-heilig-virtual-reality-headset.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Sensorama-morton-heilig-virtual-reality-headset.jpg/330px-Sensorama-morton-heilig-virtual-reality-headset.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Sensorama-morton-heilig-virtual-reality-headset.jpg/440px-Sensorama-morton-heilig-virtual-reality-headset.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sensorama-morton-heilig-virtual-reality-headset.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Sensorama was released in the 1950s.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:View-Master_with_Reel.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="692" data-file-width="806" height="189" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/View-Master_with_Reel.jpg/220px-View-Master_with_Reel.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/View-Master_with_Reel.jpg/330px-View-Master_with_Reel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/View-Master_with_Reel.jpg/440px-View-Master_with_Reel.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:View-Master_with_Reel.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/View-Master" title="View-Master">View-Master</a>, a stereoscopic visual simulator, was introduced in 1939.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The first references to the concept of virtual reality came from <a href="/wiki/Science_fiction" title="Science fiction">science fiction</a>. <a href="/wiki/Stanley_G._Weinbaum" title="Stanley G. Weinbaum">Stanley G. Weinbaum</a>'s 1935 short story "Pygmalion's Spectacles" <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gutenberg.org_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-gutenberg.org-9">[9]</a></sup> describes a goggle-based virtual reality system with holographic recording of fictional experiences, including smell and touch.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1950.E2.80.931970">1950–1970</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Virtual_reality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: 1950–1970">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Morton_Heilig" title="Morton Heilig">Morton Heilig</a> wrote in the 1950s of an "Experience Theatre" that could encompass all the senses in an effective manner, thus drawing the viewer into the onscreen activity. He built a prototype of his vision dubbed the <a href="/wiki/Sensorama" title="Sensorama">Sensorama</a> in 1962, along with five short films to be displayed in it while engaging multiple senses (sight, sound, smell, and touch). Predating digital computing, the Sensorama was a <a href="/wiki/Machine" title="Machine">mechanical device</a>. Around the same time, <a href="/wiki/Douglas_Engelbart" title="Douglas Engelbart">Douglas Engelbart</a> used computer screens as both input and output devices. In 1968, <a href="/wiki/Ivan_Sutherland" title="Ivan Sutherland">Ivan Sutherland</a>, with the help of his student <a href="/wiki/Bob_Sproull" title="Bob Sproull">Bob Sproull</a>, created what is widely considered to be the first virtual reality and <a href="/wiki/Augmented_reality" title="Augmented reality">augmented reality</a> (AR) <a href="/wiki/Head-mounted_display" title="Head-mounted display">head-mounted display</a> (HMD) system. It was primitive both in terms of <a href="/wiki/User_interface" title="User interface">user interface</a> and <a href="/wiki/Realism_(arts)" title="Realism (arts)">realism</a>, and the HMD to be worn by the user was so heavy that it had to be suspended from the ceiling. The graphics comprising the virtual environment were simple <a href="/wiki/Wire-frame_model" title="Wire-frame model">wire-frame model</a> rooms. The formidable appearance of the device inspired its name, <a href="/wiki/The_Sword_of_Damocles_(virtual_reality)" title="The Sword of Damocles (virtual reality)">The Sword of Damocles</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1970.E2.80.931990">1970–1990</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Virtual_reality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: 1970–1990">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mus%C3%A9e_M%C3%A9canique_205.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1632" data-file-width="894" height="365" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Mus%C3%A9e_M%C3%A9canique_205.JPG/200px-Mus%C3%A9e_M%C3%A9canique_205.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Mus%C3%A9e_M%C3%A9canique_205.JPG/300px-Mus%C3%A9e_M%C3%A9canique_205.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Mus%C3%A9e_M%C3%A9canique_205.JPG/400px-Mus%C3%A9e_M%C3%A9canique_205.JPG 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mus%C3%A9e_M%C3%A9canique_205.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Battlezone_(1980_video_game)" title="Battlezone (1980 video game)">Battlezone</a>, an arcade video game from 1980, used 3D vector graphics to immerse the player in a VR world.(Atari).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Also notable among the earlier <a href="/wiki/Hypermedia" title="Hypermedia">hypermedia</a> and virtual reality systems was the <a href="/wiki/Aspen_Movie_Map" title="Aspen Movie Map">Aspen Movie Map</a>, which was created at <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Institute_of_Technology" title="Massachusetts Institute of Technology">MIT</a> in 1978. The program was a crude virtual simulation of <a href="/wiki/Aspen,_Colorado" title="Aspen, Colorado">Aspen, Colorado</a> in which users could wander the streets in one of three modes: summer, winter, and polygons. The first two were based on photographs—the researchers actually photographed every possible movement through the city's street grid in both seasons—and the third was a basic 3-D model of the city. Atari founded a research lab for virtual reality in 1982, but the lab was closed after two years due to Atari Shock (<a href="/wiki/North_American_video_game_crash_of_1983" title="North American video game crash of 1983">North American video game crash of 1983</a>). However, its hired employees, such as Tom Zimmerman, Scott Fisher, Jaron Lanier and Brenda Laurel, kept their research and development on VR-related technologies. By the 1980s the term "virtual reality" was popularized by <a href="/wiki/Jaron_Lanier" title="Jaron Lanier">Jaron Lanier</a>, one of the modern pioneers of the field. Lanier had founded the company <a href="/wiki/VPL_Research" title="VPL Research">VPL Research</a> in 1985. VPL Research has developed several VR devices like the Data Glove, the Eye Phone, and the Audio Sphere. VPL licensed the Data Glove technology to Mattel, which used it to make an accessory known as the Power Glove. While the Power Glove was hard to use and not popular, at US$75, it was early affordable VR device.</p>
<p>During this time, virtual reality was not well known, though it did receive media coverage in the late 1980s. Most of its popularity came from marginal cultures, like <a href="/wiki/Cyberpunk" title="Cyberpunk">cyberpunks</a>, who viewed the technology as a potential means for social change, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Recreational_drug" title="Recreational drug">recreational drug</a> subculture, who praised virtual reality not only as a new art form, but as an entirely new frontier.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-10">[10]</a></sup> Some drug users consume drugs while using VR technologies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> The concept of virtual reality was popularized in mass media by movies such as <i><a href="/wiki/Brainstorm_(1983_film)" title="Brainstorm (1983 film)">Brainstorm</a></i> (1983) and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Lawnmower_Man_(film)" title="The Lawnmower Man (film)">The Lawnmower Man</a></i>. The VR research boom of the 1990s was accompanied by the non-fiction book <i>Virtual Reality</i> (1991) by <a href="/wiki/Howard_Rheingold" title="Howard Rheingold">Howard Rheingold</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rh_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rh-12">[12]</a></sup> The book served to demystify the subject, making it more accessible to researchers outside of the computer sphere and sci-fi enthusiasts.</p>
<p>Once the industry began to attract media coverage, some even compared the innovations in virtual reality to the Wright Brothers' pioneering invention of the airplane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-13">[13]</a></sup> In 1990, Jonathan Waldern, a VR Ph.D, demonstrates "<a href="/wiki/Virtuality_(gaming)" title="Virtuality (gaming)">Virtuality</a>" at the Computer Graphics 90 exhibition staged at London's Alexandra Palace. This new system was an arcade machine that would use a <a href="/wiki/Virtual_reality_headset" title="Virtual reality headset">virtual reality headset</a> to immerse players. <i>CyberEdge</i> and <i>PCVR</i>, two VR industry magazines, started to publish in the early 1990s. However, most ideas about VR remained theoretical due to the limited computing power available at the time. The extremely high cost of the technology made it impossible for consumers to adopt. When the Internet became widely available, this became the technology focus for most people. The VR industry mainly provided VR devices for medical, flight simulation, automobile industry design, and military training purposes from 1970 to 1990.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1990.E2.80.932000">1990–2000</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Virtual_reality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: 1990–2000">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="metadata plainlinks ambox ambox-content" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ambox_question.svg"><img alt="Ambox question.svg" data-file-height="40" data-file-width="40" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Ambox_question.svg/40px-Ambox_question.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Ambox_question.svg/60px-Ambox_question.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Ambox_question.svg/80px-Ambox_question.svg.png 2x" width="40"/></a></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>possibly contains <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research#Synthesis_of_published_material_that_advances_a_position" title="Wikipedia:No original research">previously unpublished synthesis</a> of published material that conveys ideas not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">attributable</a> to the original sources.</b> <span class="hide-when-compact">Relevant discussion may be found on the <a href="/wiki/Talk:Virtual_reality#.23" title="Talk:Virtual reality">talk page</a>.</span> <small><i>(February 2015)</i></small> <small class="hide-when-compact"><i>(<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>In 1991, <a href="/wiki/Sega" title="Sega">Sega</a> announced the <a href="/wiki/Sega_VR" title="Sega VR">Sega VR</a> headset for <a href="/wiki/Arcade_game" title="Arcade game">arcade games</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mega_Drive" title="Mega Drive">Mega Drive</a> console. It used <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liquid_crystal_display" title="Liquid crystal display">LCD</a> screens in the visor, stereo headphones, and inertial sensors that allowed the system to <a href="/wiki/Tracking_system" title="Tracking system">track</a> and react to the movements of the user's head.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Horowitz_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Horowitz-14">[14]</a></sup> In the same year, <a href="/wiki/Virtuality_(gaming)" title="Virtuality (gaming)">Virtuality</a> launched and went on to become the first mass-produced, networked, multiplayer VR entertainment system. It was released in many countries, including a dedicated VR arcade at <a href="/wiki/Embarcadero_Center" title="Embarcadero Center">Embarcadero Center</a> in <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco" title="San Francisco">San Francisco</a>. Costing up to $73,000 per multi-pod Virtuality system, they featured headsets and exoskeleton gloves that gave one of the first "immersive" VR experiences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> Antonio Medina, a MIT graduate and NASA scientist, designed a virtual reality system to "drive" Mars rovers from Earth in apparent real time despite the substantial delay of Mars-Earth-Mars signals. The system, termed "Computer-Simulated Teleoperation" as published by Rand, is an extension of virtual reality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1991, <a href="/wiki/Carolina_Cruz-Neira" title="Carolina Cruz-Neira">Carolina Cruz-Neira</a>, <a href="/wiki/Daniel_J._Sandin" title="Daniel J. Sandin">Daniel J. Sandin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_A._DeFanti" title="Thomas A. DeFanti">Thomas A. DeFanti</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Electronic_Visualization_Laboratory" title="Electronic Visualization Laboratory">Electronic Visualization Laboratory</a> created the <a href="/wiki/Cave_automatic_virtual_environment" title="Cave automatic virtual environment">first cubic immersive room</a>, replacing goggles by a multi-projected environment where people can see their body and other people around. In that same year, <i><a href="/wiki/Computer_Gaming_World" title="Computer Gaming World">Computer Gaming World</a></i> predicted "Affordable VR by 1994".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-engler1992_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-engler1992-17">[17]</a></sup> By 1994, Sega released the <a href="/wiki/Sega_VR" title="Sega VR">Sega VR</a>-1 motion simulator <a href="/wiki/Arcade_game" title="Arcade game">arcade attraction</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> in <a href="/wiki/SegaWorld" title="SegaWorld">SegaWorld</a> <a href="/wiki/Amusement_arcade" title="Amusement arcade">amusement arcades</a>. It was able to track head movement and featured <a href="/wiki/3D_computer_graphics" title="3D computer graphics">3D polygon graphics</a> in <a href="/wiki/Stereoscopic_video_game" title="Stereoscopic video game">stereoscopic 3D</a>, powered by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sega_Model_1" title="Sega Model 1">Sega Model 1</a> <a href="/wiki/Arcade_system_board" title="Arcade system board">arcade system board</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> Also in 1994 Apple released QuickTime VR. A widely available product for interacting with VR models.</p>
<p>A year later, the artist <a href="/wiki/Maurice_Benayoun" title="Maurice Benayoun">Maurice Benayoun</a> created the first VR artwork connecting in real time 2 continents: the "Tunnel under the Atlantic" between the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pompidou_Centre" title="Pompidou Centre">Pompidou Centre</a> in Paris and the Museum of Contemporary Art in <a href="/wiki/Montreal" title="Montreal">Montreal</a>. The installation included dynamic real time 3d modeling, video chat, spatialized sound and AI content management. The <a href="/wiki/Virtual_Boy" title="Virtual Boy">Virtual Boy</a> was created by <a href="/wiki/Nintendo" title="Nintendo">Nintendo</a> and was released in Japan on July 21, 1995 and in North America on August 15, 1995.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> Also in 1995, a group in Seattle created public demonstrations of a <a href="/wiki/Cave_automatic_virtual_environment" title="Cave automatic virtual environment">"CAVE-like"</a> 270 degree immersive projection room called the Virtual Environment Theater, produced by entrepreneurs Chet Dagit and Bob Jacobson.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Then in 1996 the same system was shown in tradeshow exhibits sponsored by <a href="/wiki/Netscape" title="Netscape">Netscape</a> Communications, and championed by Jim Barksdale, for the first time showing VR connected to the Internet with World Wide Web content feeds embedded in <a href="/wiki/VRML" title="VRML">VRML</a> 3D virtual world models. Forte released the <a href="/wiki/VFX1_Headgear" title="VFX1 Headgear">VFX1</a>, a PC-powered virtual reality headset in 1995, which was supported by games including <i><a href="/wiki/Descent_(video_game)" title="Descent (video game)">Descent</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Star_Wars:_Dark_Forces" title="Star Wars: Dark Forces">Star Wars: Dark Forces</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/System_Shock" title="System Shock">System Shock</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Quake_(video_game)" title="Quake (video game)">Quake</a></i>. In 1999, entrepreneur <a href="/wiki/Philip_Rosedale" title="Philip Rosedale">Philip Rosedale</a> formed <a href="/wiki/Linden_Lab" title="Linden Lab">Linden Lab</a> with an initial focus on the development of hardware that would enable computer users to be fully immersed in a 360 degree virtual reality experience. In its earliest form, the company struggled to produce a commercial version of "The Rig," which was realized in prototype form as a clunky steel contraption with several computer monitors that users could wear on their shoulders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> That vision soon morphed into the software-based, 3D virtual world <a href="/wiki/Second_Life" title="Second Life">Second Life</a>.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Oculus_Rift_-_Developer_Version_-_Front.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3670" data-file-width="5496" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Oculus_Rift_-_Developer_Version_-_Front.jpg/220px-Oculus_Rift_-_Developer_Version_-_Front.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Oculus_Rift_-_Developer_Version_-_Front.jpg/330px-Oculus_Rift_-_Developer_Version_-_Front.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Oculus_Rift_-_Developer_Version_-_Front.jpg/440px-Oculus_Rift_-_Developer_Version_-_Front.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Oculus_Rift_-_Developer_Version_-_Front.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A 2013 developer version of <a href="/wiki/Oculus_Rift" title="Oculus Rift">Oculus Rift</a> from <a href="/wiki/Oculus_VR" title="Oculus VR">Oculus VR</a>, a company <a href="/wiki/Facebook" title="Facebook">Facebook</a> acquired in 2014 for $2 billion</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2000.E2.80.932016">2000–2016</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Virtual_reality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: 2000–2016">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Google-Cardboard.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2400" data-file-width="2730" height="193" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Google-Cardboard.jpg/220px-Google-Cardboard.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Google-Cardboard.jpg/330px-Google-Cardboard.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Google-Cardboard.jpg/440px-Google-Cardboard.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Google-Cardboard.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The affordable and accessible <a href="/wiki/Google_Cardboard" title="Google Cardboard">Google Cardboard</a> standard.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 2001, SAS3 or SAS Cube became the first PC based cubic room, developed by Z-A Production (<a href="/wiki/Maurice_Benayoun" title="Maurice Benayoun">Maurice Benayoun</a>, David Nahon), Barco, Clarté, installed in Laval France in April 2001. The SAS library gave birth to Virtools VRPack. By 2007, <a href="/wiki/Google" title="Google">Google</a> introduced <a href="/wiki/Google_Street_View" title="Google Street View">Street View</a>, a service that shows panoramic views of an increasing number of worldwide positions such as roads, indoor buildings and rural areas. It also features a stereoscopic 3D mode, introduced in 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> In 2010, <a href="/wiki/Palmer_Luckey" title="Palmer Luckey">Palmer Luckey</a>, who later went on to found <a href="/wiki/Oculus_VR" title="Oculus VR">Oculus VR</a>, designed the first prototype of the <a href="/wiki/Oculus_Rift" title="Oculus Rift">Oculus Rift</a>. This prototype, built on a shell of another virtual reality headset, displayed only 2-D images and was noticeably cumbersome to wear. However, it boasted a 90-degree field of vision that was previously unseen anywhere in the market at the time. This initial design would later serve as a basis from which the later designs came.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2013, <a href="/wiki/Nintendo" title="Nintendo">Nintendo</a> filed a <a href="/wiki/Patent" title="Patent">patent</a> for the concept of using VR technology to produce a more realistic <a href="/wiki/Stereoscopy" title="Stereoscopy">3D effect</a> on a 2D television. A camera on the TV tracks the viewer's location relative to the TV, and if the viewer moves, everything on the screen reorients itself appropriately. "For example, if you were looking at a forest, you could shift your head to the right to discover someone standing behind a tree."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> In July 2013, Guild Software's <a href="/wiki/Vendetta_Online" title="Vendetta Online">Vendetta Online</a> was widely reported as the first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MMORPG" title="MMORPG">MMORPG</a> to support the <a href="/wiki/Oculus_Rift" title="Oculus Rift">Oculus Rift</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> making it potentially the first persistent online world with native support for a consumer virtual reality headset. Since 2013, there have been several virtual reality devices that seek to enter the market to complement Oculus Rift to enhance the game experience. One, <a href="/wiki/Virtuix_Omni" title="Virtuix Omni">Virtuix Omni</a>, is based on the ability to move in a three dimensional environment through an omnidirectional treadmill.</p>
<p>On March 25, 2014, <a href="/wiki/Facebook" title="Facebook">Facebook</a> purchased a company that makes virtual reality headsets, <a href="/wiki/Oculus_VR" title="Oculus VR">Oculus VR</a>, for $2 billion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> In that same month, <a href="/wiki/Sony" title="Sony">Sony</a> announced Project Morpheus (its code name for <a href="/wiki/PlayStation_VR" title="PlayStation VR">PlayStation VR</a>), a virtual reality headset for the <a href="/wiki/PlayStation_4" title="PlayStation 4">PlayStation 4</a> video game console.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> Google announces <a href="/wiki/Google_Cardboard" title="Google Cardboard">Cardboard</a>, a do-it-yourself stereoscopic viewer for smartphones. The user places her smartphone in the cardboard holder, which she wears on her head. In 2015, the Kickstarter campaign for Gloveone, a pair of gloves providing motion tracking and haptic feedback, was successfully funded, with over $150,000 in contributions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>In February–March 2015, <a href="/wiki/HTC" title="HTC">HTC</a> partnered with <a href="/wiki/Valve_Corporation" title="Valve Corporation">Valve Corporation</a> announced their virtual reality headset <a href="/wiki/HTC_Vive" title="HTC Vive">HTC Vive</a> and controllers, along with their tracking technology called Lighthouse, which utilizes "base stations" mounted to the wall above the user's head in the corners of a room for positional tracking of the Vive headset and its motion controllers using infrared light.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-engadget-steamvr_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-engadget-steamvr-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-verge-gdcsteamvr_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-verge-gdcsteamvr-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-verge-valvevr_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-verge-valvevr-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> The company announced its plans to release the Vive to the public in April 2016 on December 8, 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> Units began shipping on April 5, 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<p>In July 2015, <a href="/wiki/OnePlus" title="OnePlus">OnePlus</a> became the first company to launch a product using virtual reality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> They used VR as the platform to launch their second flagship device the OnePlus 2, first viewable using an app on the Google Play Store,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> then on YouTube.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> The launch was viewable using OnePlus Cardboard, based on the Google's own Cardboard platform. The whole VR launch had a runtime of 33 minutes, and was viewable in all countries. Also in 2015, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jaunt_(Company)" title="Jaunt (Company)">Jaunt</a>, a startup company developing cameras and a <a href="/wiki/Cloud_computing" title="Cloud computing">cloud</a> distribution platform, whose content will be accessible using an <a href="/wiki/Mobile_app" title="Mobile app">app</a>, reached $100 million in funding from such sources as <a href="/wiki/The_Walt_Disney_Company" title="The Walt Disney Company">Disney</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Madison_Square_Garden_Company" title="The Madison Square Garden Company">Madison Square Garden</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> On April 27, 2016, <a href="/wiki/Mojang" title="Mojang">Mojang</a> announced that <a href="/wiki/Minecraft" title="Minecraft">Minecraft</a> is now playable on the <a href="/wiki/Samsung_Gear_VR" title="Samsung Gear VR">Gear VR</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> Minecraft is still being developed for the <a href="/wiki/Oculus_Rift" title="Oculus Rift">Oculus Rift</a> headset but a separate version was released to the Oculus Store for use with the Gear VR. This version is similar to the Pocket Edition of Minecraft.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Use">Use</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Virtual_reality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Use">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Education_and_training">Education and training</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Virtual_reality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Education and training">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:VR-Helm.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1960" data-file-width="3008" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/VR-Helm.jpg/220px-VR-Helm.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/VR-Helm.jpg/330px-VR-Helm.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/VR-Helm.jpg/440px-VR-Helm.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:VR-Helm.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">U.S. Navy</a> personnel using a VR parachute training simulator.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Research has being done on learning in virtual reality, as its immersive qualities may enhance learning. VR is used by trainers to provide learners with a virtual environment where they can develop their skills without the real-world consequences of failing. <a href="/wiki/Thomas_A._Furness_III" title="Thomas A. Furness III">Thomas A. Furness III</a> was one of the first to develop the use of VR for military training when, in 1982, he presented the Air Force with his first working model of a virtual flight simulator he called the Visually Coupled Airborne Systems Simulator (VCASS). By the time he started his work on VCASS, aircraft were becoming increasingly complicated to handle and virtual reality provided a better solution to previous training methods. Furness attempted to incorporate his knowledge of human visual and auditory processing to create a virtual interface that was more intuitive to use. The second phase of his project, which he called the "Super Cockpit," was even more advanced, with high resolution graphics (for the time) and a responsive display. Furness is often credited as a pioneer in virtual reality for this research.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-10">[10]</a></sup> VR plays an important role in combat training for the military. It allows the recruits to train under a controlled environment where they are to respond to different types of combat situations. A fully immersive virtual reality that uses <a href="/wiki/Head-mounted_display" title="Head-mounted display">head-mounted display</a> (HMD), data suits, data glove, and VR weapon are used to train for combat. This setup allows the training's reset time to be cut down, and allows more repetition in a shorter amount of time. The fully immersive training environment allows the soldiers to train through a wide variety of terrains, situations and scenarios.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Virtual_Reality_Simulator_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Virtual_Reality_Simulator-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:A_soldier_with_the_Royal_Netherlands_Army_sits_on_the_gunner_station_while_training_in_the_Training_Support_Activity_Europe%27s_Virtual_Clearance_Training_Suite_as_part_of_the_European_Union%27s_Battle_group_140221-A-FS311-062.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4912" data-file-width="7360" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/A_soldier_with_the_Royal_Netherlands_Army_sits_on_the_gunner_station_while_training_in_the_Training_Support_Activity_Europe%27s_Virtual_Clearance_Training_Suite_as_part_of_the_European_Union%27s_Battle_group_140221-A-FS311-062.jpg/200px-thumbnail.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/A_soldier_with_the_Royal_Netherlands_Army_sits_on_the_gunner_station_while_training_in_the_Training_Support_Activity_Europe%27s_Virtual_Clearance_Training_Suite_as_part_of_the_European_Union%27s_Battle_group_140221-A-FS311-062.jpg/300px-thumbnail.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/A_soldier_with_the_Royal_Netherlands_Army_sits_on_the_gunner_station_while_training_in_the_Training_Support_Activity_Europe%27s_Virtual_Clearance_Training_Suite_as_part_of_the_European_Union%27s_Battle_group_140221-A-FS311-062.jpg/400px-thumbnail.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:A_soldier_with_the_Royal_Netherlands_Army_sits_on_the_gunner_station_while_training_in_the_Training_Support_Activity_Europe%27s_Virtual_Clearance_Training_Suite_as_part_of_the_European_Union%27s_Battle_group_140221-A-FS311-062.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A headscreen-wearing soldier sits at a gunner station while learning in a Virtual Training Suite.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>VR is also used in flight simulation for the Air Force where people are trained to be pilots. The simulator would sit on top of a hydraulic lift system that reacts to the user inputs and events. When the pilot steer the aircraft, the module would turn and tilt accordingly to provide <a href="/wiki/Haptic_technology" title="Haptic technology">haptic feedback</a>. The flight simulator can range from a fully enclosed module to a series of computer monitors providing the pilot's point of view. The most important reasons on using simulators over learning with a real aircraft are the reduction of transference time between land training and real flight, the safety, economy and absence of pollution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> By the same token, virtual driving simulations are used to train tank drivers on the basics before allowing them to operate the real vehicle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Virtual_Reality_Training_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Virtual_Reality_Training-46">[46]</a></sup> Finally, the same goes for truck driving simulators, in which Belgian firemen are for example trained to drive in a way that prevents as much damage as possible. As these drivers often have less experience than other truck drivers, virtual reality training allows them to compensate this. In the near future, similar projects are expected for all drivers of priority vehicles, including the police.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>Medical personnel are able to train through VR to deal with a wider variety of injuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Virtual_reality_combat_training_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Virtual_reality_combat_training-48">[48]</a></sup> An experiment was performed by sixteen surgical residents where eight of them went through <a href="/wiki/Cholecystectomy" title="Cholecystectomy">laparoscopic cholecystectomy</a> through VR training. They then came out 29% faster at <a href="/wiki/Gallbladder" title="Gallbladder">gallbladder</a> dissection than the controlled group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Virtual_Reality_Training2_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Virtual_Reality_Training2-49">[49]</a></sup> With the increased commercial availability of certified training programs for basic skills training in VR environments, students have the ability to familiarize themselves with necessary skills in a corrective and repetitive environment; VR is also proven to help students familiarize themselves with skills not specific to any particular procedure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> VR application was used to train road crossing skills in children. It proved to be rather successful. However some students with autistic spectrum disorders after such training might be unable to distinguish virtual from real. As a result, they may attempt quite dangerous road crossings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Video_games">Video games</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Virtual_reality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Video games">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PlaystationVR_(29014205246).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3648" data-file-width="5472" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/PlaystationVR_%2829014205246%29.jpg/220px-PlaystationVR_%2829014205246%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/PlaystationVR_%2829014205246%29.jpg/330px-PlaystationVR_%2829014205246%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/PlaystationVR_%2829014205246%29.jpg/440px-PlaystationVR_%2829014205246%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PlaystationVR_(29014205246).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Playstation_VR" title="Playstation VR">Playstation VR</a> headset used in video games</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:HAPTIKA_.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2048" data-file-width="949" height="367" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/HAPTIKA_.jpg/170px-HAPTIKA_.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/HAPTIKA_.jpg/255px-HAPTIKA_.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/HAPTIKA_.jpg/340px-HAPTIKA_.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:HAPTIKA_.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A person wearing haptic feedback devices, which enable her to feel elements in the virtual world.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The use of graphics, sound and input technology in <a href="/wiki/Video_game" title="Video game">video games</a> can be incorporated into VR. Several Virtual Reality head mounted displays (HMD) were released for gaming during the early-mid 1990s. These included the <a href="/wiki/Virtual_Boy" title="Virtual Boy">Virtual Boy</a> developed by <a href="/wiki/Nintendo" title="Nintendo">Nintendo</a>, the iGlasses developed by Virtual I-O, the Cybermaxx developed by Victormaxx and the <a href="/wiki/VFX1_Headgear" title="VFX1 Headgear">VFX1 Headgear</a> developed by <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Forte_Technologies&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Forte Technologies (page does not exist)">Forte Technologies</a>. Other modern examples of narrow VR for gaming include the <a href="/wiki/Wii_Remote" title="Wii Remote">Wii Remote</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Kinect" title="Kinect">Kinect</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/PlayStation_Move" title="PlayStation Move">PlayStation Move</a>/<a href="/wiki/PlayStation_Eye" title="PlayStation Eye">PlayStation Eye</a>, all of which track and send motion input of the players to the game console somewhat accurately. Several companies are working on a new generation of VR headsets: <a href="/wiki/Oculus_Rift" title="Oculus Rift">Oculus Rift</a> is a head-mounted display for gaming purposes developed by <a href="/wiki/Oculus_VR" title="Oculus VR">Oculus VR</a>, an American technology company that was acquired for US$2 billion by Facebook in 2014. One of its rivals was named by <a href="/wiki/Sony" title="Sony">Sony</a> as <a href="/wiki/PlayStation_VR" title="PlayStation VR">PlayStation VR</a> (codenamed Morpheus), which requires a PS4 instead of a PC to run. In 2015, <a href="/wiki/Valve_Corporation" title="Valve Corporation">Valve Corporation</a> announced their partnership with <a href="/wiki/HTC" title="HTC">HTC</a> to make a VR headset capable of tracking the exact position of its user in a 4.5 by 4.5 meters area, the <a href="/wiki/HTC_Vive" title="HTC Vive">HTC Vive</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> All these virtual reality headsets are tethered headsets that use special lenses to magnify and stretch a 5.7-inch screen (in the case of Morpheus) across the field of vision. There are more gaming VR headsets in development, each with its own special abilities. <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=StarVR&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="StarVR (page does not exist)">StarVR</a> offers a 210° field of view, whereas <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=FOVE&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="FOVE (page does not exist)">FOVE</a> tracks the position of your eyes as an input method.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Entertainment">Entertainment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Virtual_reality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Entertainment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Virtual reality technologies are also widely used in entertainment and have reflected in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Virtual_reality_simulators" title="Virtual reality simulators">virtual reality simulators</a> (VR simulators/ VR amusements/ VR game simulators/ vr motion simulators).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fine_arts">Fine arts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Virtual_reality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Fine arts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/David_Em" title="David Em">David Em</a> was the first fine artist to create navigable virtual worlds in the 1970s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> His early work was done on mainframes at <a href="/wiki/Information_International,_Inc." title="Information International, Inc.">Information International, Inc.</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jet_Propulsion_Laboratory" title="Jet Propulsion Laboratory">Jet Propulsion Laboratory</a>, and <a href="/wiki/California_Institute_of_Technology" title="California Institute of Technology">California Institute of Technology</a>. <a href="/wiki/Jeffrey_Shaw" title="Jeffrey Shaw">Jeffrey Shaw</a> explored the potential of VR in fine arts with early works like <i>Legible City</i> (1989), <i>Virtual Museum</i> (1991), and <i>Golden Calf</i> (1994). Canadian artist <a href="/wiki/Char_Davies" title="Char Davies">Char Davies</a> created immersive VR art pieces <i>Osmose</i> (1995) and <i>Ephémère</i> (1998). <a href="/wiki/Maurice_Benayoun" title="Maurice Benayoun">Maurice Benayoun</a>'s work introduced metaphorical, philosophical or political content, combining VR, network, generation and intelligent agents, in works like <i>Is God Flat?</i> (1994), "Is the Devil Curved?" (1995), <i><a href="/wiki/Maurice_Benayoun" title="Maurice Benayoun">The Tunnel under the Atlantic</a></i> (1995), and <i>World Skin, a Photo Safari in the Land of War</i> (1997). Other pioneering artists working in VR have include <a href="/wiki/Knowbotic_Research" title="Knowbotic Research">Knowbotic Research</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rebecca_Allen_(artist)" title="Rebecca Allen (artist)">Rebecca Allen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Perry_Hoberman" title="Perry Hoberman">Perry Hoberman</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Engineering">Engineering</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Virtual_reality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Engineering">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The use of 3D <a href="/wiki/Computer-aided_design" title="Computer-aided design">computer-aided design</a> (CAD) data was limited by 2D monitors and paper printouts until the mid-to-late 1990s, when video projectors, 3D tracking, and computer technology enabled a renaissance in the use 3D CAD data in virtual reality environments. With the use of active shutter glasses and multi-surface projection units immersive engineering was made possible by companies like VRcom and <a class="extiw" href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/ICIDO" title="de:ICIDO">IC.IDO</a>. Virtual reality has been used in automotive, aerospace, and ground transportation original equipment manufacturers (OEMs) in their product engineering and manufacturing engineering . Virtual reality adds more dimensions to <a href="/wiki/Virtual_prototyping" title="Virtual prototyping">virtual prototyping</a>, product building, assembly, service, performance use-cases. This enables engineers from different disciplines to view their design as its final product. Engineers can view the virtual bridge, building or other structure from any angle. As well, some computer models allow engineers to test their structure's resistance to winds, weight, and other elements. Immersive VR engineering systems enable engineers, management and investors to see virtual prototypes prior to the availability of any physical prototypes.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Heritage_and_archaeology">Heritage and archaeology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Virtual_reality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Heritage and archaeology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The first use of a VR presentation in a heritage application was in 1994, when a museum visitor interpretation provided an interactive "walk-through" of a 3D reconstruction of <a href="/wiki/Dudley_Castle" title="Dudley Castle">Dudley Castle</a> in England as it was in 1550. This consisted of a computer controlled laserdisc-based system designed by British-based engineer Colin Johnson. The system was featured in a conference held by the British Museum in November 1994, and in the subsequent technical paper, <i>Imaging the Past – Electronic Imaging and Computer Graphics in Museums and Archaeology</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> Virtual reality enables heritage sites to be recreated extremely accurately, so that the recreations can be published in various media.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> The original sites are often inaccessible to the public or, due to the poor state of their preservation, hard to picture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> This technology can be used to develop virtual replicas of caves, natural environment, old towns, monuments, sculptures and archaeological elements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Architectural_design">Architectural design</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Virtual_reality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Architectural design">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:A_visitor_at_Mozilla_Berlin_Hackshibition_trying_Oculus_Rift_virtual_reality_experience.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="338" data-file-width="600" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/A_visitor_at_Mozilla_Berlin_Hackshibition_trying_Oculus_Rift_virtual_reality_experience.gif/220px-A_visitor_at_Mozilla_Berlin_Hackshibition_trying_Oculus_Rift_virtual_reality_experience.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/A_visitor_at_Mozilla_Berlin_Hackshibition_trying_Oculus_Rift_virtual_reality_experience.gif/330px-A_visitor_at_Mozilla_Berlin_Hackshibition_trying_Oculus_Rift_virtual_reality_experience.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/A_visitor_at_Mozilla_Berlin_Hackshibition_trying_Oculus_Rift_virtual_reality_experience.gif/440px-A_visitor_at_Mozilla_Berlin_Hackshibition_trying_Oculus_Rift_virtual_reality_experience.gif 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:A_visitor_at_Mozilla_Berlin_Hackshibition_trying_Oculus_Rift_virtual_reality_experience.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A visitor at Mozilla Berlin Hackshibition trying Oculus Rift virtual reality experience on Firefox.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>One of the first recorded uses of virtual reality in architecture was in the late 1980s when the University of North Carolina modeled its Sitterman Hall, home of its computer science department, in a virtual environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-13">[13]</a></sup> Several companies, including IrisVR and Floored, Inc., provide software or services that allow architectural design firms and various clients in the real estate industry to tour virtual models of proposed building designs. IrisVR currently provides software that allows users to convert design files created in CAD programs like SketchUp and Revit into files viewable with an Oculus Rift, HTC Vive, or a smartphone "in one click," without the need for complex tiered workflows or knowledge of game engines such as Unity3D.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> Floored, meanwhile, manually constructs and refines Rift-viewable 3D models in-house from either CAD files for un-built designs or physical scans of already built, brick-and-mortar buildings, and provides clients with access to its own viewing software, which can be used with either an Oculus Rift or a standard 2D web browser, afterward.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<p>VR software products like these can provide a number of benefits to architects and their clients. During the design process, architects can use VR to experience the designs they are working on before they are built. Seeing a design in VR can give architect a correct sense of scale and proportion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> Having an interactive VR model also eliminates the need to make physical miniatures to demonstrate a design to clients or the public. Later on, after a building is constructed, developers and owners can create a VR model of a space that allows potential buyers or tenants to tour a space in VR, even if real-life circumstances make a physical tour unfeasible. For instance, if the owner of an apartment building has a VR model of a space while the building is under construction, she can begin showing and renting the units before they are even ready to be occupied. Furthermore, this sort of showing can be conducted over any distance, as long as the potential customer has access to a VR setup (or, even, with the help of <a href="/wiki/Google_Cardboard" title="Google Cardboard">Google Cardboard</a> or a similar phone-based VR headset, nothing but a smartphone.)</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Urban_design">Urban design</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Virtual_reality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Urban design">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:A_Coved_land_development.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1006" data-file-width="1897" height="117" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/A_Coved_land_development.JPG/220px-A_Coved_land_development.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/A_Coved_land_development.JPG/330px-A_Coved_land_development.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/A_Coved_land_development.JPG/440px-A_Coved_land_development.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:A_Coved_land_development.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A land development plan using Prefurbia, a 4th generation design system.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 2010, 3D virtual reality was beginning to be used for urban regeneration, planning and transportation projects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> In 2007, development began on a virtual reality software which took design coordinate geometry used by land surveyors and civil engineers and incorporated precision spatial information created automatically by the lines and curves typically shown on subdivision plats and land surveying plans. These precise spatial areas cross referenced color and texture to an item list. The item list contained a set of controls for <a href="/wiki/3D_rendering" title="3D rendering">3D rendering</a>, such as water reflective surfaces or building height. The land surface in software to create a contour map uses a digital terrain model (DTM). By 2010, prototype software was developed for LandMentor, a technology to automate the process leading from design to virtualization. The first beta users in 2011 were able drape the design or survey data over the digital terrain to create data structures that were passed into a video game engine. The engine was able to create a virtual interactive world. The software was improved to implement 3D models from other free or commercially sold software to create a more realistic virtual reality.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:A_Coved_Streetscape.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1003" data-file-width="1883" height="117" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/A_Coved_Streetscape.JPG/220px-A_Coved_Streetscape.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/A_Coved_Streetscape.JPG/330px-A_Coved_Streetscape.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/A_Coved_Streetscape.JPG/440px-A_Coved_Streetscape.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:A_Coved_Streetscape.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A streetscape with homes, showing architectural shaping and blending</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Therapy">Therapy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Virtual_reality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Therapy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Virtual_reality_therapy" title="Virtual reality therapy">Virtual reality therapy</a></div>
<p>The primary use of VR in a therapeutic role is its application to various forms of exposure therapy, including <a href="/wiki/Phobia" title="Phobia">phobia</a> treatments.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Theme_parks">Theme parks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Virtual_reality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Theme parks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Since 2015, virtual reality has been installed onto a number of roller coasters, including <a href="/wiki/Galactica_(roller_coaster)" title="Galactica (roller coaster)">Galactica</a> at <a href="/wiki/Alton_Towers" title="Alton Towers">Alton Towers</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_New_Revolution_(roller_coaster)" title="The New Revolution (roller coaster)">The New Revolution</a> at <a href="/wiki/Six_Flags_Magic_Mountain" title="Six Flags Magic Mountain">Six Flags Magic Mountain</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alpenexpress" title="Alpenexpress">Alpenexpress</a> at <a href="/wiki/Europa-Park" title="Europa-Park">Europapark</a>, amongst others.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Concerts">Concerts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Virtual_reality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Concerts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Assembled_Google_Cardboard_VR_mount.jpg"><img alt="Virtual Reality" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3072" data-file-width="4608" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Assembled_Google_Cardboard_VR_mount.jpg/220px-Assembled_Google_Cardboard_VR_mount.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Assembled_Google_Cardboard_VR_mount.jpg/330px-Assembled_Google_Cardboard_VR_mount.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Assembled_Google_Cardboard_VR_mount.jpg/440px-Assembled_Google_Cardboard_VR_mount.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Assembled_Google_Cardboard_VR_mount.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Assembled Google Cardboard VR</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Oslo_Spektrum" title="Oslo Spektrum">Oslo Spektrum</a> on May the 3rd 2016, Norwegian pop band <a href="/wiki/A-ha" title="A-ha">a-ha</a> cleared away their normal stage-production to give room for a very different concert performance in collaboration with Void, a <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norwegian</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Computational" title="Computational">computational</a> design studio working in the intersection between <a href="/wiki/Design" title="Design">design</a>, <a href="/wiki/Architecture" title="Architecture">architecture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Art" title="Art">art</a> and <a href="/wiki/Technology" title="Technology">technology</a>. The collaboration resulted in a unique one-of-a-kind concert with advanced <a href="/wiki/Scenography" title="Scenography">scenography</a> using 360 virtual reality technology. It used <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Movement_sensors&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Movement sensors (page does not exist)">movement sensors</a> that reacted to the band members' movements, voices and instruments. <a href="/wiki/Stereo_camera" title="Stereo camera">3D cameras</a>, 20000 lines of codes, 1000 square meters of projection film and massive projectors was set up into a visual show that made the <a href="/wiki/Oslo_Spektrum" title="Oslo Spektrum">Oslo Spektrum</a> arena in <a href="/wiki/Oslo" title="Oslo">Oslo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a> into a light installation and visual experience that unfolded live for the audience instead of a pre programmed sequence. The stereoscopic VR-experience was made available for Android users directly through a YouTube app and also made available for iPhone users and other platforms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Retail">Retail</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Virtual_reality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Retail">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Lowe%27s" title="Lowe's">Lowe's</a>, <a href="/wiki/IKEA" title="IKEA">IKEA</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wayfair" title="Wayfair">Wayfair</a> and other retailers have developed systems that allow their products to be seen in virtual reality, to give consumers a better idea of how the product will fit into their home, or to allow the consumer to get a better look at the product from home.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> Consumers looking at digital photos of the products can "turn" the product around virtually, and see it from the side or the back. This enables customers to get a better sense of how a product looks and about its features, than with the typical single picture used in traditional paper catalogues. The retail travel industry such as Cruise About and <a href="/wiki/Flight_Centre" title="Flight Centre">Flight Centre</a> have been adopting VR to enable customer in their stores to view cruise cabins, tourism content, and hotel rooms prior to booking.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Charity">Charity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Virtual_reality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Charity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Non-profit organisations such as <a href="/wiki/Amnesty_International" title="Amnesty International">Amnesty International</a>, <a href="/wiki/UNICEF" title="UNICEF">UNICEF</a>, and <a href="/wiki/World_Wide_Fund_for_Nature" title="World Wide Fund for Nature">World Wide Fund for Nature</a> (WWF) have started using virtual reality to bring potential supporters closer to their work, effectively bringing distant social, political and environmental issues and projects to members of the public in immersive ways not possible with traditional media. Panoramic 360 views of conflict in Syria<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> and face to face encounters with CGI tigers in Nepal<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> have been used in experiential activations and shared online to both educate and gain financial support for such charitable work.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Exercise_and_fitness">Exercise and fitness</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Virtual_reality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Exercise and fitness">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Certain companies are using VR to target the fitness industry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Film">Film</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Virtual_reality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Film">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Many companies, including <a href="/wiki/GoPro" title="GoPro">GoPro</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nokia" title="Nokia">Nokia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Samsung" title="Samsung">Samsung</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ricoh" title="Ricoh">Ricoh</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nikon" title="Nikon">Nikon</a>, develop <a href="/wiki/Omnidirectional_camera" title="Omnidirectional camera">omnidirectional cameras</a>, also known as 360-degree cameras or VR cameras, that have the ability to record in all directions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> These cameras are used to create images and videos that can be viewed in VR. (See <a href="/wiki/VR_photography" title="VR photography">VR photography</a>.) Films produced for VR permit the audience to view the entire environment in every scene, creating an interactive viewing experience. Production companies, such as <a href="/wiki/Fox_Searchlight_Pictures" title="Fox Searchlight Pictures">Fox Searchlight Pictures</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Skybound" title="Skybound">Skybound</a>, utilize VR cameras to produce films that are interactive in VR. Fox Searchlight, Oculus and <a href="/wiki/Samsung_Gear_VR" title="Samsung Gear VR">Samsung Gear VR</a> collaborated on a project titled "Wild – The Experience", starring Reese Witherspoon. The VR film was presented at the <a href="/wiki/Consumer_Electronics_Show" title="Consumer Electronics Show">Consumer Electronics Show</a> as well as the <a href="/wiki/Sundance_Film_Festival" title="Sundance Film Festival">Sundance Film Festival</a> in January 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> On December 8, 2015, the production company <a href="/wiki/Skybound_Entertainment" title="Skybound Entertainment">Skybound</a> announced their VR thriller titled "Gone". In collaboration with the VR production company WEVR, and <a href="/wiki/Samsung_Gear_VR" title="Samsung Gear VR">Samsung Gear VR</a>, the 360-degree video series was released on January 20, 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Media">Media</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Virtual_reality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Media">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Media companies such as <a href="/wiki/Paramount_Pictures" title="Paramount Pictures">Paramount Pictures</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Disney" title="Disney">Disney</a> have applied VR into marketing campaigns creating interactive forms of media. In October 2014 Paramount Pictures, in collaboration with the media production company <a href="/wiki/Framestore" title="Framestore">Framestore</a>, created a VR experience utilizing the <a href="/wiki/Oculus" title="Oculus">Oculus</a> DK2. The experience was dubbed a "time sensitive adventure in space" that took place in a portion of the Endurance space ship from the film "Interstellar." The experience was available to the public at limited AMC theater locations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> In May 2016, Disney released a VR experience titled Disney Movies VR on Valve Corporation's <a href="/wiki/Steam" title="Steam">Steam</a> software, free for download. The experience allows users to interact with the characters and worlds from the Disney, Marvel, and Lucasfilm universes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pornography">Pornography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Virtual_reality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Pornography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Pornographic studios such as <a href="/wiki/Naughty_America" title="Naughty America">Naughty America</a>, <a href="/wiki/BaDoinkVR" title="BaDoinkVR">BaDoinkVR</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kink.com" title="Kink.com">Kink</a> have applied VR into their products since late 2015 or early 2016. The clips and videos are shot from an angle that resembles POV-style porn.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Marketing">Marketing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Virtual_reality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Marketing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Virtual Reality has the potential to completely replace many forms of digital marketing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> Marketing strategies in small and middle sized enterprises, ever more frequently include virtual reality in their ad concepts, POS material and budgets. Virtual Reality presents a unique opportunity for advertisers to reach a completely immersed audience.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sports_viewing">Sports viewing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Virtual_reality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Sports viewing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In September 2016, Agon announced that the upcoming <a href="/wiki/World_Chess_Championship" title="World Chess Championship">World Chess Championship</a> match between <a href="/wiki/Magnus_Carlsen" title="Magnus Carlsen">Magnus Carlsen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sergey_Karjakin" title="Sergey Karjakin">Sergey Karjakin</a> would be "the first in any sport to be broadcast in 360-degree virtual reality."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_fiction">In fiction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Virtual_reality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: In fiction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span">This section may <b>require <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Cleanup" title="Wikipedia:Cleanup">cleanup</a></b> to meet Wikipedia's <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style">quality standards</a>. The specific problem is: This section doesn't distinguish between <a href="/wiki/Simulated_reality" title="Simulated reality">simulated reality</a> and virtual reality. The difference to SR should be explained and entries not featuring VR removed <small><i>(June 2015)</i></small> <small class="hide-when-compact"><i>(<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></span></td>
