<small class="hide-when-compact"><i>(<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>Many <a href="/wiki/Science_fiction" title="Science fiction">science fiction</a> books and films have imagined characters being "trapped in virtual reality" or entering into virtual reality. A comprehensive and specific fictional model for virtual reality was published in 1935 in the short story "Pygmalion's Spectacles"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gutenberg.org_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-gutenberg.org-9">[9]</a></sup> by <a href="/wiki/Stanley_G._Weinbaum" title="Stanley G. Weinbaum">Stanley G. Weinbaum</a>. A more modern work to use this idea was <a href="/wiki/Daniel_F._Galouye" title="Daniel F. Galouye">Daniel F. Galouye</a>'s novel <i><a href="/wiki/Simulacron-3" title="Simulacron-3">Simulacron-3</a></i>, which was made into a German teleplay titled <i>Welt am Draht</i> ("World on a Wire") in 1973. Other science fiction books have promoted the idea of virtual reality as a partial, but not total, substitution for the misery of reality, or have touted it as a method for creating <a href="/wiki/Virtual_world" title="Virtual world">virtual worlds</a> in which one may escape from Earth. <a href="/wiki/Stanis%C5%82aw_Lem" title="Stanisław Lem">Stanisław Lem</a>'s 1961 story "I (Profesor Corcoran)", translated in English as "Further Reminiscences of Ijon Tichy I",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> dealt with a scientist who created a number of computer-simulated people living in a virtual world. Lem further explored the implications of what he termed "phantomatics" in his nonfictional 1964 treatise <i><a href="/wiki/Summa_Technologiae" title="Summa Technologiae">Summa Technologiae</a></i>. The <a href="/wiki/Piers_Anthony" title="Piers Anthony">Piers Anthony</a> novel <i><a href="/wiki/Killobyte" title="Killobyte">Killobyte</a></i> follows the story of a paralyzed cop trapped in a virtual reality game by a hacker, whom he must stop to save a fellow trapped player slowly succumbing to insulin shock.</p>
<p>A number of other popular fictional works use the concept of virtual reality. These include <a href="/wiki/William_Gibson" title="William Gibson">William Gibson</a>'s 1984 <i><a href="/wiki/Neuromancer" title="Neuromancer">Neuromancer</a></i>, which defined the concept of cyberspace, and his 1994 <i><a href="/wiki/Virtual_Light" title="Virtual Light">Virtual Light</a></i>, where a presentation viewable in VR-like goggles was the <a href="/wiki/MacGuffin" title="MacGuffin">MacGuffin</a>. Other examples are <a href="/wiki/Neal_Stephenson" title="Neal Stephenson">Neal Stephenson</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Snow_Crash" title="Snow Crash">Snow Crash</a></i>, in which he made extensive reference to the term avatar to describe one's representation in a virtual world, and <a href="/wiki/Rudy_Rucker" title="Rudy Rucker">Rudy Rucker</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Hacker_and_the_Ants" title="The Hacker and the Ants">The Hacker and the Ants</a></i>, in which programmer Jerzy Rugby uses VR for robot design and testing. The <a href="/wiki/Otherland" title="Otherland">Otherland</a> series of 4 novels by <a href="/wiki/Tad_Williams" title="Tad Williams">Tad Williams</a>, published from 1996 to 2001 and set in the 2070s, shows a world where the <a href="/wiki/Internet" title="Internet">Internet</a> has become accessible via virtual reality. The <i><a href="/wiki/Doctor_Who" title="Doctor Who">Doctor Who</a></i> serial "<a href="/wiki/The_Deadly_Assassin" title="The Deadly Assassin">The Deadly Assassin</a>", first broadcast in 1976, introduced a dream-like computer-generated reality, known as <a href="/wiki/Matrix_(Doctor_Who)" title="Matrix (Doctor Who)">the Matrix</a>. British BBC2 sci-fi series <i><a href="/wiki/Red_Dwarf" title="Red Dwarf">Red Dwarf</a></i> featured a virtual reality game titled "Better Than Life", in which the main characters had spent many years connected. <a href="/wiki/Saban_Capital_Group" title="Saban Capital Group">Saban</a>'s syndicated superhero television series <i><a href="/wiki/VR_Troopers" title="VR Troopers">VR Troopers</a></i> also made use of the concept. The <a href="/wiki/Holodeck" title="Holodeck">holodeck</a> featured in <i><a href="/wiki/Star_Trek:_The_Next_Generation" title="Star Trek: The Next Generation">Star Trek: The Next Generation</a></i> is one of the best known examples of virtual reality in popular culture, including the ability for users to interactively modify scenarios in real time with a natural language interface. The depiction differs from others in the use of a physical room rather than a neural interface or headset.</p>
<p>The <i><a href="/wiki/.hack" title=".hack">.hack</a></i> multimedia franchise is based on a virtual reality <a href="/wiki/Massively_multiplayer_online_role-playing_game" title="Massively multiplayer online role-playing game">MMORPG</a> dubbed "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_World_(.hack)" title="The World (.hack)">The World</a>". The French animated series <i><a href="/wiki/Code_Lyoko" title="Code Lyoko">Code Lyoko</a></i> is based on the virtual world of <i><a href="/wiki/Code_Lyoko" title="Code Lyoko">Lyoko</a></i> and the <a href="/wiki/Internet" title="Internet">Internet</a>. In 2009, British <a href="/wiki/Digital_radio" title="Digital radio">digital radio</a> station <a href="/wiki/BBC_Radio_4_Extra" title="BBC Radio 4 Extra">BBC Radio 7</a> broadcast <i><a href="/wiki/Planet_B" title="Planet B">Planet B</a></i>, a science-fiction drama set in a virtual world. <i>Planet B</i> was the largest ever commission for an original drama programme.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> The 2012 series <i><a href="/wiki/Sword_Art_Online" title="Sword Art Online">Sword Art Online</a></i> involves the concept of a virtual reality MMORPG of the same name, with the possibility of dying in real life when a player dies in the game. Also, in its 2014 sequel, <i>Sword Art Online II</i>, the idea of bringing a virtual character into the real world via mobile cameras is posed; this concept is used to allow a bedridden individual to attend public school for the first time.<i><a href="/wiki/Accel_World" title="Accel World">Accel World</a></i> (2012) expands the concept of virtual reality using the game <i>Brain Burst</i>, a game which allows players to gain and receive points to keep accelerating; accelerating is when an individual's brain perceives the images around them 1000 times faster, heightening their sense of awareness.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Motion_pictures">Motion pictures</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Virtual_reality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Motion pictures">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Worldskin-01.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="450" data-file-width="600" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Worldskin-01.jpg/220px-Worldskin-01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Worldskin-01.jpg/330px-Worldskin-01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Worldskin-01.jpg/440px-Worldskin-01.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>

<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Worldskin-01.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
World Skin (1997), <a href="/wiki/Maurice_Benayoun" title="Maurice Benayoun">Maurice Benayoun</a>'s virtual reality interactive installation</div>
</div>
</div>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Rainer_Werner_Fassbinder" title="Rainer Werner Fassbinder">Rainer Werner Fassbinder</a>'s 1973 film <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Welt_am_Draht" title="Welt am Draht">Welt am Draht</a></i>, based on Daniel F. Galouye's novel Simulacron-3, shows a virtual reality simulation <i>inside</i> another virtual reality simulation</li>
<li>In 1983, the <a href="/wiki/Natalie_Wood" title="Natalie Wood">Natalie Wood</a> / <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Walken" title="Christopher Walken">Christopher Walken</a> film <i><a href="/wiki/Brainstorm_(1983_film)" title="Brainstorm (1983 film)">Brainstorm</a></i> revolved around the production, use, and misuse of a VR device.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Total_Recall_(1990_film)" title="Total Recall (1990 film)">Total Recall</a></i>, directed by Paul Verhoeven and based on the <a href="/wiki/Philip_K._Dick" title="Philip K. Dick">Philip K. Dick</a> story "We Can Remember It for You Wholesale"</li>
<li>A VR-like system, used to record and play back dreams, figures centrally in <a href="/wiki/Wim_Wenders" title="Wim Wenders">Wim Wenders</a>' 1991 film <i><a href="/wiki/Until_the_End_of_the_World" title="Until the End of the World">Until the End of the World</a></i>.</li>
<li>The 1992 film <i><a href="/wiki/The_Lawnmower_Man_(film)" title="The Lawnmower Man (film)">The Lawnmower Man</a></i> tells the tale of a research scientist who uses a VR system to jumpstart the mental and physical development of his mentally handicapped gardener.</li>
<li>The 1993 film <i><a href="/wiki/Arcade_(film)" title="Arcade (film)">Arcade</a></i> is centered around a new virtual reality game (from which the film gets its name) that actively traps those who play it inside its world.</li>
<li>The 1995 film <i><a href="/wiki/Strange_Days_(film)" title="Strange Days (film)">Strange Days</a></i> is a science-fiction thriller about a fictional virtual reality trend in which users buy illegal VR recordings of criminal offences recorded from the offender's <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Point_of_view" title="Point of view">point of view</a> (POV).</li>
<li>The 1995 film <i><a href="/wiki/Johnny_Mnemonic_(film)" title="Johnny Mnemonic (film)">Johnny Mnemonic</a></i> has the main character Johnny (played by <a href="/wiki/Keanu_Reeves" title="Keanu Reeves">Keanu Reeves</a>) use virtual reality goggles and <a href="/wiki/Brain%E2%80%93computer_interface" title="Brain–computer interface">brain–computer interfaces</a> to access the Internet and extract encrypted information in his own brain.</li>
<li>The 1995 film <i><a href="/wiki/Virtuosity" title="Virtuosity">Virtuosity</a></i> has <a href="/wiki/Russell_Crowe" title="Russell Crowe">Russell Crowe</a> as a virtual reality <a href="/wiki/Serial_killer" title="Serial killer">serial killer</a> name SID 6.7 (Sadistic, Intelligent and Dangerous) who is used in a simulation to train real-world <a href="/wiki/Police_officer" title="Police officer">police officer</a>, but manages to escape into the real world.</li>
<li>The 1999 film <i><a href="/wiki/The_Thirteenth_Floor" title="The Thirteenth Floor">The Thirteenth Floor</a></i> is an adaptation of Daniel F. Galouye's novel <i>Simulacron-3</i>, and tells about two virtual reality simulations, one in another.</li>
<li>In 1999, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Matrix" title="The Matrix">The Matrix</a></i> and later sequels explored the possibility that our world is actually a vast virtual reality (or more precisely, <a href="/wiki/Simulated_reality" title="Simulated reality">simulated reality</a>) created by artificially intelligent machines.</li>
<li><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/EXistenZ" title="EXistenZ">eXistenZ</a></i> (1999), by David Cronenberg, in which level switches occur so seamlessly and numerously that at the end of the movie it is difficult to tell whether the main characters are back in "reality".</li>
<li>In the film <i><a href="/wiki/Avatar_(2009_film)" title="Avatar (2009 film)">Avatar</a></i>, the humans are hooked up via advanced technologies with <a href="/wiki/Avatar" title="Avatar">avatars</a>, enabling the avatars to remotely perform the actions of the humans.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Surrogates" title="Surrogates">Surrogates</a></i> (2009) is based on a <a href="/wiki/Brain%E2%80%93computer_interface" title="Brain–computer interface">brain–computer interface</a> that allows people to control realistic humanoid robots, giving them full sensory feedback.</li>
<li>The 2010 <a href="/wiki/Science_fiction_film" title="Science fiction film">science fiction</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thriller_film" title="Thriller film">thriller</a> film <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inception_(film)" title="Inception (film)">Inception</a></i> is about a professional thief who <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Corporate_espionage" title="Corporate espionage">steals information</a> by infiltrating the subconscious. He creates artificial thoughts that are so realistic that once they are implanted in a person's mind, the person thinks these are his own thoughts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-synop_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-synop-85">[85]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Concerns_and_challenges">Concerns and challenges</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Virtual_reality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Concerns and challenges">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Virtual_reality_sickness" title="Virtual reality sickness">Virtual reality sickness</a></div>
<p>There are certain health and safety considerations of virtual reality. For example, a number of unwanted symptoms have been caused by prolonged use of virtual reality,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> and these may have slowed proliferation of the technology. Most virtual reality systems come with consumer warnings. Virtual reality sickness (also known as cybersickness) occurs when a person's exposure to a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Virtual_environment" title="Virtual environment">virtual environment</a> causes symptoms that are similar to <a href="/wiki/Motion_sickness" title="Motion sickness">motion sickness</a> symptoms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-one_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-one-87">[87]</a></sup> The most common symptoms are general discomfort, headache, stomach awareness, nausea, vomiting, pallor, sweating, fatigue, drowsiness, disorientation, and apathy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-two_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-two-88">[88]</a></sup> Other symptoms include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Postural_instability" title="Postural instability">postural instability</a> and retching.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-two_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-two-88">[88]</a></sup> Virtual reality sickness is different from motion sickness in that it can be caused by the visually-induced perception of self-motion; real self-motion is not needed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-one_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-one-87">[87]</a></sup> It is also different from <a href="/wiki/Simulator_sickness" title="Simulator sickness">simulator sickness</a>; non-virtual reality simulator sickness tends to be characterized by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oculomotor" title="Oculomotor">oculomotor</a> disturbances, whereas virtual reality sickness tends to be characterized by disorientation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-three_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-three-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
<p>In addition, there are social, conceptual, and philosophical considerations and implications associated with the use of virtual reality. What the phrase "virtual reality" means or refers to can be ambiguous. In the book <i>The Metaphysics of Virtual Reality</i> by <a href="/wiki/Michael_R._Heim" title="Michael R. Heim">Michael R. Heim</a>, seven different concepts of virtual reality are identified: simulation, interaction, artificiality, immersion, <a href="/wiki/Telepresence" title="Telepresence">telepresence</a>, <a href="/wiki/Immersion_(virtual_reality)" title="Immersion (virtual reality)">full-body immersion</a>, and network communication. There has been an increase in interest in the potential social impact of new technologies, such as virtual reality. In the book <i>Infinite Reality: Avatars, Eternal Life, New Worlds, and the Dawn of the Virtual Revolution,</i> Blascovich and Bailenson review the literature on the psychology and sociology behind life in virtual reality. Mychilo S. Cline's book <i>Power, Madness, and Immortality: The Future of Virtual Reality</i>, argues that virtual reality will lead to a number of important changes in human life and activity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> He argues that virtual reality will be integrated into daily life and activity, and will be used in various human ways. Another such speculation has been written up on how to reach ultimate happiness via virtual reality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> He also argues that techniques will be developed to influence human behavior, <a href="/wiki/Interpersonal_communication" title="Interpersonal communication">interpersonal communication</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cognition" title="Cognition">cognition</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> As we spend more and more time in virtual space, there would be a gradual "migration to virtual space", resulting in important changes in economics, worldview, and culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> Philosophical implications of VR are discussed in books, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philip_Zhai" title="Philip Zhai">Philip Zhai</a>'s <i>Get Real: A Philosophical Adventure in Virtual Reality</i> (1998) and <i>Digital Sensations: Space, Identity and Embodiment in Virtual Reality</i> (1999), written by Ken Hillis.</p>
<p>Virtual reality technology faces a number of challenges, most of which involve <a href="/wiki/Motion_sickness" title="Motion sickness">motion sickness</a> and technical matters. Users might become disoriented in a purely virtual environment, causing balance issues; computer latency might affect the simulation, providing a less-than-satisfactory end-user experience; the complicated nature of head-mounted displays and input systems such as specialized gloves and boots may require specialized training to operate, and navigating the non-virtual environment (if the user is not confined to a limited area) might prove dangerous without external sensory information. In January 2014, <a href="/wiki/Michael_Abrash" title="Michael Abrash">Michael Abrash</a> gave a talk on VR at Steam Dev Days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> He listed all the requirements necessary to establish <a href="/wiki/Immersion_(virtual_reality)#Presence" title="Immersion (virtual reality)">presence</a> and concluded that a great VR system will be available in 2015 or soon after. While the visual aspect of VR is close to being solved, he stated that there are other areas of VR that need solutions, such as 3D audio, haptics, body tracking, and input. However, <a href="/wiki/3D_audio_effect" title="3D audio effect">3D audio effects</a> exist in games and simulate the <a href="/wiki/Head-related_transfer_function" title="Head-related transfer function">head-related transfer function</a> of the listener (especially using headphones). Examples include <a href="/wiki/Environmental_Audio_Extensions" title="Environmental Audio Extensions">Environmental Audio Extensions</a> (EAX), <a href="/wiki/DirectSound" title="DirectSound">DirectSound</a> and <a href="/wiki/OpenAL" title="OpenAL">OpenAL</a>. VR audio developer <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Varun_Nair&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Varun Nair (page does not exist)">Varun Nair</a> points out that from a design perspective, sound for VR is still very much an open book. Many of the game audio design principles, especially those related to <a href="/wiki/First-person_shooter" title="First-person shooter">FPS</a> games, crumble in virtual reality. He encourages more <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sound_designer" title="Sound designer">sound designers</a> to get involved in virtual reality audio to experiment and push VR audio forward.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> There have been rising concerns that with the advent of virtual reality, some users may experience <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Virtual_reality_addiction" title="Virtual reality addiction">virtual reality addiction</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Commercial_industries">Commercial industries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Virtual_reality&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Commercial industries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="metadata plainlinks ambox ambox-style ambox-Prose" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">

<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>contains <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Embedded_lists" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Embedded lists">embedded lists</a> that may be better presented using <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Prose" title="Wikipedia:Prose">prose</a>.</b> <span class="hide-when-compact">You can help by converting the list or lists to prose, if <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Embedded_lists" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Embedded lists">appropriate</a>. <a href="/wiki/Help:Editing" title="Help:Editing">Editing help</a> is available.</span> <small><i>(February 2015)</i></small> <small class="hide-when-compact"><i>(<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></span></td>

</table>
<p>The companies working in the virtual reality sector fall broadly into three categories of involvement: hardware (making headsets and input devices specific to VR), software (producing software for interfacing with the hardware or for delivering content to users) and content creation (producing content, whether interactive or passive storylines, games, and artificial worlds, for consumption and exploration with VR hardware).</p>
<div class="div-col columns column-count column-count-2" style="-moz-column-count: 2; -webkit-column-count: 2; column-count: 2;">
